
State Space Models & Kalman FilterOpen sidebarState Space Models & Kalman FilterGPT-4summarize: The discovery and development of state space models and the Kalman filter have
their roots in the dynamical and control systems research communities. The Kalman
filter was proposed initially as an alternative to the Wiener-Kolmogorov estimation
theory after Kalman applied state variables to the filtering problem. Soon after its
conception, the Kalman filter was associated with significant real-data application
such as the Apollo project at the Ames Research Centre at NASA. The Kalman
filter was soon discovered by the statistics and econometrics research communities,
which were able to exploit the flexibility of the state space formulation in the quest to
describe dynamic systems and to use the filter as a powerful forecasting framework.
Bayesian inference and forecasting were key areas of development in the 1980s
and 1990s. By that time, the state space models and the Kalman filter were found
to be extremely popular in a wide range of applied science, including statistics,
engineering, economics, environmetrics and biology, to name but a few. Partly due
to the availability of computer advancement, over the last 20 years, there has been
an increasing interest in multivariate linear Gaussian, non-linear and non-Gaussian
modelling and related state space methods. This is facilitated by deploying Bayesian
computation, in particular sequential Monte Carlo and Markov chain Monte Carlo
methods, but also by other estimation procedures such as the unscented Kalman
filter and related algorithms.
The principle idea of this book is to bring together the above models with their
statistical inference and make them available to a broad audience. Teaching time
series and state space modelling for over 15 years at the University of Sheffield
has motivated me to take up this book project. During these years, I have met and
collaborated with a number of applied scientists sharing a keen interest in state space
models. Some of these models can be generated by a natural phenomenon such
as met in engineering or environmetrics and some can be generated by a socioeconomic structure such as in finance and its applications. The book is written
from a statisticianâs perspective, and it uses a number of data sets to illustrate
the methods from a wide range of disciplines. The first six chapters discuss state
space methods and Bayesian inference for general use; the last two chapters of the
book focus on finance and dynamical systems. On one hand, economics and finance1 / 1ChatGPTSummary:State space models and the Kalman filter originated from dynamical and control systems research. (Cumulative Distribution Function): It describes the probability that a random variable takes on a value less than or equal to x.DGLM (Dynamic Generalised Linear Model): An extension of the general linear model that allows for observations to depend on an underlying state that evolves over time.EKF (Extended Kalman Filter): An extension of the Kalman filter that linearizes about an estimate of the current mean and covariance to handle nonlinearities.EM (Expectation Maximisation): An algorithm used to find the maximum likelihood estimates of parameters when there's missing data or latent variables.FFBS (Forward Filtering Backward Sampling): An algorithm used in time series analysis, particularly with state space models, for smoothing and obtaining posterior distributions of states.i.i.d. (Independent and Identically Distributed): A collection of random variables that have the same probability distribution and are independent of each other.GARCH (Generalised Autoregressive Conditional Heteroskedastic Models): A time series model that accounts for changing variances over time, commonly used in financial data.GLM (Generalised Linear Model): A flexible generalization of the linear regression that allows for response variables that have error distribution models other than a normal distribution.MCMC (Markov Chain Monte Carlo): A class of algorithms for sampling from a probability distribution based on constructing a Markov chain that has the desired distribution as its equilibrium distribution.MAD (Mean Absolute Deviation): A measure of dispersion in a dataset, calculated by taking the average of the absolute differences between each data point and the mean.MGARCH (Multivariate Generalised Autoregressive Conditional Heteroskedastic Models): An extension of the GARCH model that considers multiple time series and accounts for changing variances and covariances over time.Each of these terms and models offers a unique approach or insight into statistical modeling and analysis, particularly in the domain of time series forecasting and analysis.1 / 1MIMO Multiple input multiple output (dynamic system)

It's a common metric for the accuracy of estimators or predictors.MSSE (Mean Squared Standardised Error): The mean of the squared differences between predicted values and observed values, normalized by some standard.MSOP (Multivariate Scaled Observation Precision Model): A model that incorporates multiple observations and scales them based on their precision.OLS (Ordinary Least Squares): A method used in linear regression to estimate the parameters of a linear model by minimizing the sum of the squared residuals.p.d.f. (Probability Density Function): For continuous random variables, it represents the likelihood of a particular outcome.PID (Proportional Integral Derivative): A type of controller used in control systems to regulate processes. It combines three control actions: proportional, integral, and derivative.PF-I (Particle Filter Algorithm I): The standard particle filter, an algorithm for implementing a recursive Bayesian filter by Monte Carlo simulations.PF-II (Particle Filter Algorithm II): Also known as the Liu and West particle filter, an enhanced version of the standard particle filter.p.m.f. (Probability Mass Function): For discrete random variables, it gives the probability that a discrete random variable is exactly equal to some value.RLS (Recursive Least Squares): A method used for recursively updating the coefficients of a linear model to minimize the sum of squared residuals.SISO (Single Input Single Output): Refers to systems with one input and one output.SOP (Scaled Observational Precision Model): A model that scales observations based on their precision.TRMS (Twin Rotor Multi-Input Multi-Output System): A system involving two rotors and functioning as a MIMO dynamic system.UKF (Unscented Kalman Filter): An approach to the nonlinear estimation problem that avoids the need to compute the Jacobian, used to handle the nonlinearities present in the problem.WAR (Whishart Autoregressive Process): A type of stochastic process that can model multivariate time-varying volatilities.WN (White Noise): A random signal or process with a flat spectral density, meaning it has the same intensity at different frequencies.Each of these terms and models provides specific insights or techniques in the domain of control systems, statistical modeling, and time series analysis.1 / 1Chapter 1
State Space Models
This chapter introduces time series, the state space model (although a more formal
treatment is given in Chap. 3) and Kalman filtering. We start by defining and giving
some examples of time series data. Section 1.2 discusses a data driven problem from
hydro-dynamics, which motivates our first encounter with the state space model.
To appreciate the wealth of applications that the state space model holds, Sect. 1.3
provides several examples from environmetrics, navigation, economics and physics.
Section 1.4 gives a historical account of the Kalman filter and the chapter concludes
by giving a brief content description.
1.1 Introduction
1.1.1 Time Series
In many subject areas, such as engineering, economics, biology, environmetrics,
data collected are frequently collected over time. Such data, known as time series,
may arise as the result of the data collection process (we may collect data in a
daily, or hourly frequency) and interest is focused in understanding the dynamics
of such data as well as forecasting future time series values. Examples of time
series include: daily prices of financial assets, monthly marriage figures, quarterly
product sales, daily temperatures in a particular city, annual precipitation levels of a
lake and so forth. In all these examples, the time in which the data are collected is
important, as it introduces a particular correlation or dependence structure between
measurements or observations. In order to understand such data, it is necessary
to consider statistical models that take into account time in the aforementioned
dependence structure. The study of such models is known as time series analysis
and has been discussed in many textbooks, see e.g. Brockwell and Davis (1991),
Shumway and Stoffer (2017) and Lindsey (2004).

To establish notation, we refer to time as t and to the time series observation at t
as yt or as y(t) (see below). In most situations t will belong to a discrete set (such as
the natural numberst = 1, 2, 3 ...) and in this respect the collection of observations
yt , for all t = 1, 2,..., denoted by {yt, t = 1, 2,...} or simply {yt} defines a
discrete-time time series. For example, t may represent days, months or years (as
in examples above). In some cases t belongs to a continuous set (e.g. the closed
interval [0, 1]); then the time series at t is denoted by y(t) (to make explicit that t is
continuous) and so we may write {y(t), t â [0, 1]} or {y(t)} for a continuous-time
time series. In this book we will primarily study discrete-time time series, unless
otherwise stated. The objectives of time series analysis are
1. to build statistical models (known as time series models) that describe and
understand the dynamics of observed time series data,
2. based on an observed collection of data, to forecast future values of the time
series.
Both (1) and (2) adopt a time series model, but while (1) aims at finding a model
that describes a collection of data, (2) adopts the model in order to forecast future
data.
A general class of time series models and one that this book is focused on, known
as state space models, suggests that at each time t the observations yt are related
to the states at time t, which in turn are related to the states at t â 1. Thus, the
observation is a function of the states, and the way in which the states move in their
space, hence the name state space (time series) models.1 / 1ChatGPTChapter 1: State Space Models SummaryMain Topic: Introduction to the concept and application of state space models in time series analysis.Time Series Data: This refers to a set of data points gathered over specific intervals of time. The primary characteristic of time series data is that the sequence matters since the timing and order of the data points introduce a correlation or dependence structure. The study of models that account for this time-based structure is called time series analysis. Notable references in this area include works by Brockwell and Davis (1991), Shumway and Stoffer (2017), and Lindsey (2004).Notation: The chapter establishes a notation system wherein time is represented by ttt and observations at time ttt are represented as ytytyt or y(t)y(t)y(t). The book mainly focuses on discrete-time series where time belongs to a specific set (like natural numbers). However, continuous-time series, where time spans a continuous range, are also mentioned.Objectives of Time Series Analysis:Develop statistical models, termed as time series models, to interpret the dynamics of observed time series data.Utilize these models to predict future values in the series.State Space Models: A unique class of time series models wherein observations ytytyt at each time ttt are related to the states at the same time. These states at time ttt are also linked to states at time tâ1t â 1tâ1. This implies that an observation is determined by the states and the manner in which these states evolve over time, hence the term "state space."Chapter Structure:Introduction to Time Series: Defines what time series data is and emphasizes its importance across various fields such as engineering, economics, biology, and environmetrics. It also highlights the need for models that take time into account due to the inherent correlation between data points.Historical Perspective of Kalman Filter: Touches upon the historical significance of the Kalman filter in state space models.Examples from Various Domains: Discusses real-world applications of state space models in areas like environmetrics, navigation, economics, and physics, showing the model's versatility.Introduction to State Space Models: Introduces the concept of state space models as a significant type of time series model, emphasizing how observations and states interrelate over time.The chapter serves as a foundation for understanding state space models and their importance in studying and predicting time series data.1 / 11.1.2 Examples of Time Series Data

which the reader is referred to http://www.lme.com/. The data of Fig. 1.1, which
exclude bank holidays and weekends, show initially some random fluctuation,
followed by an upward linear trend (MarchâApril), followed by a linear fall
(MayâJune), followed by some random fluctuation (JuneâJuly), followed by
some increasing and then decreasing trend (AugustâSeptember), and finally
followed by a linear trend (October).
â¢ Seasonal (Quarter temperatures at Sheffield).

Park, Sheffield, UK, for unspecified years. This data set shows clear evidence of
seasonality (or cyclic variation), as the values of the 1st Quarter of each year are
similar (lower values in the figure) and of course they are such due to the effect
of the winter months in the first quarter. Likewise, the third quarter of each year
is responsible for the higher temperature values, being influenced by the summer

Both examples underscore the importance of understanding the inherent structures within time series data.1 / 11.2 Water Tank Dynamics and the State Space Model
In this section we describe a mechanism that generates a linear state space model.
We consider a simple experiment used to measure the level of water in a tank.
Figure 1.3 shows a water tank, which allows a constant flow of water entering the
tank from the left side with constant flow rate 6 litres per min and leaving the tank on
the bottom right side at flow rate 5 litres per min. The objective of the experiment
is to measure the level L of the water in the course of time. The first observation
we make is that the level is not constant as the water coming to the tank creates
disturbance affecting the level of the water. Secondly, the water leaving the tank has
lower flow rate than the flow rate entering the tank. Assuming water mass per litre is
constant, this means that in the long run we expect the level to increase by 1 litre per
min. If there was no disturbance due to water entering the tank, the volume in the
tank at time t is Lt = L0+t, where  is the length of the base of the tank and L0 is
the initial level of the water (assumed to be higher than the higher point of the right
leave point of the tank). The above equation can be written as Lt = Ltâ1 + 1,
starting from L1 = L0 + 1. With the disturbance generated by the water falling
into the tank we postulate that Lt â Ltâ1 +1. This in words says that the volume 
of the tank at time t (in min) is close to the volume of the tank at time t â 1 plus
1 litre. This can be modelled by introducing a sequence of random variables Î¶t , so
that
Lt = Ltâ1 + 1 + Î¶t, (1.1)
where Î¶t is a i.i.d. sequential of random variables with zero mean and some variance
Z. For this model we have E(Lt) = E(Ltâ1) + 1/ and Var(Lt) â¥ Var(Ltâ1).
Consider now that an observer is measuring the level Lt . This may be achieved
automatically by placing a float in the tank. This will create an extra source of
uncertainty on the level of the water. A possible model for the observed level yt
suggests that yt is expected to be equal to the actual (unobserved) level Lt inflated
by noise  t , caused by the float. Hence the measurement equation is
yt = Lt +  t, (1.2)
where  t is an i.i.d. sequence of random variables with zero mean and variance Ï2.
Initially a value for L0 (the level as time t = 0 should be set); alternatively it
is possible to consider that L0 is a random variable, if we wish to specify an initial
distribution for it (this specification will be a useful consideration, if the engineer
is not certain about the initial value L0). Measurement model (1.2) together the
evolution model (1.1) and the initial specification of L0 define a state space model.
The unobserved signal Lt is the state at time t and the observation yt is a linear
function of Lt . Again collecting water float measurement data y1,...,yn we can
estimate the unknown water tank float at each time Lt .

Before we proceed with the definition of the general state space model, we
need to establish some notation. Suppose that x represents a p-dimensional column
vector, i.e.

element xij at position (i, j ). As is evident from the above, we use boldface to
distinguish a matrix from a scalar or a vector. In a probabilistic setting, we deal with
random variables (for univariate variables) and random vectors (for multivariate

for more flexibility. It is assumed that the sequences { t} and {Î¶t} are independent
(i.e.  t is independent of  s and Î¶t is independent of Î¶s, for any t  = s) and that

The section uses the example of a water tank's dynamics to illustrate the concepts and structures behind the state space model. By observing how the water level changes with the inflow, outflow, and disturbances, we can derive a mathematical representation. This model allows for the understanding of state dynamics and measurements, two key components in state space modeling.1 / 1If Gaussian distributions are assumed for  t , Î¶t and for Î²0, model (1.3) is known
as Gaussian linear state space model, and it can be described by
yt | Î²t â¼ N(xt Î²t, Ï2) and Î²t | Î²tâ1 â¼ N(FtÎ²tâ1,Z), (1.4)
where yt | Î²t denotes the conditional distribution of yt given Î²t (a formal definition
is given in Chap. 2), Ï2 is the variance of  t and Z is the covariance matrix of Î¶t .
Expression (1.4) allows us to extend the Gaussian linear state space model (1.4)
to non-linear and non-Gaussian state space models. Indeed, we will say that the
time series {yt} is generated by a general (including linear Gaussian and nonlinear and non-Gaussian) state space model, if yt can be described by the following
distributions
p(yt | Î²t), p(Î²t | Î²tâ1) and p(Î²0). (1.5)
This, very general, model setting assumes a distribution of yt , given some states,
a distribution of Î²t , given the previous state Î²tâ1 and an initial state distribution
p(Î²0). It is implicitly assumed that given Î²t , yt is conditionally independent of
past observations and states ytâ1, ytâ2,... and of Î²tâ1, Î²tâ2,..., but also of future
observations and states yt+1, yt+2,... and Î²t+1, Î²t+2,...; in other words, the
present state Î²t holds all information from past and future data and states relevant
to the understanding and knowledge of yt . Likewise, given Î²tâ1, Î²t is conditionally
independent of Î²tâ2, Î²tâ3,.... Simply put, we say that given the present (state at
time t), the past and the future are conditionally independent. More information
about this independence structure can be found in West and Harrison (1997). It is
worth noting that the above description fits the purposes of state space models for
discrete and roughly equally spaced observed data. Gaussian Linear State Space Model:
If the error terms Î´tÎ´_tÎ´tâ, Î¶tÎ¶_tÎ¶tâ and the initial state Î²0Î²_0Î²0â are all assumed to follow Gaussian distributions, then the model as expressed in equation (1.3) is termed the Gaussian linear state space model. It can be mathematically defined by the expressions:

A key feature of this model is its conditional independence structure:Given the present state Î²tÎ²_tÎ²tâ, yty_tytâ is conditionally independent of prior observations and states (like ytâ1y_{tâ1}ytâ1â, ytâ2y_{tâ2}ytâ2â, etc.) and of future observations and states (like yt+1y_{t+1}yt+1â, yt+2y_{t+2}yt+2â, etc.).Similarly, when considering Î²tâ1Î²_{tâ1}Î²tâ1â, Î²tÎ²_tÎ²tâ is conditionally independent of Î²tâ2Î²_{tâ2}Î²tâ2â, Î²tâ3Î²_{tâ3}Î²tâ3â, and so forth.This reflects the idea that the present state captures all relevant information from past and future data, making past and future data conditionally independent when considering the current state.4. More Insights:
The conditional independence structure, as described, aligns with the objectives of state space models for data that are discrete and approximately equidistant in their observations. However, there are more advanced models for continuous-time data. Examples and discussions on these models can be found in various sources, with one such model detailed in Section 1.3.4 and further explored in the context of dynamical systems in Section 8.4.Reference: West and Harrison (1997) provide more in-depth understanding about the conditional independence structure in state space models.Summary:
The Gaussian Linear State Space Model, as its name suggests, employs Gaussian distributions for its error terms and initial state. This model can be broadened to accommodate non-linear and non-Gaussian variations, leading to the general state space model. A fundamental aspect of these models is the principle of conditional independence, where the present state encapsulates all the necessary information, making past and future data conditionally independent of the present.1 / 11.3 Examples of State Space Models
This section describes some situations motivated by real-life problems, which can
be modelled with a state space model. It illustrates some of the many subject areas
that state space models find application.
1.3.1 Forecasting Air-Pollution Levels
Air pollution consists of the introduction of chemicals, particulate matter and
biological materials into the atmosphere, causing severe damage to the environment.
Many of the main air pollutants are contributing to the greenhouse effect, which is
considered to be the main human made factor that affects climate change.
Nitric oxide (NO), one of the most prominent air pollutants, is emitted from
high temperature combustion, and also produced naturally during thunderstorms by
electrical discharge. Figure 1.4 shows NO levels (in mg/m3) together with levels
of % humidity, mean daily temperature (in â¦C), and wind speed (in m/s); the
measurements of these variables are collected daily covering January to December
2001 and they are provided by one of the sensors sites of the air-pollution networks
of Athens.
One of the objectives is to be able to use the covariates (here denoted by x1tâ
humidity, x2tâtemperature and x3tâwind speed) in order to forecast future values
of the NO levels (denoted by yt). Such information may be vital in issuing warning
messages to the community, should the pollution levels be expected to rise, e.g.
by preventing old people, and in particular those with respiratory related health
problems, access particular areas of the city. Another objective may be to establish
pollution trends and dynamics, so as to assess whether anti-pollution measures work
and assist policy makers.
A first model is a simple regression model of x1t, x2t, x3t on the response variable
yt , given by

where  t is an independent sequence, following a Gaussian distribution with zero
mean and some variance Ï2, i.e.  t â¼ N(0, Ï2). The coefficient Î²0 is the intercept
and Î²1, Î²2, Î²3 are the coefficients of the covariates x1t, x2t, x3t . We can fit this
model by using standard regression methods, see e.g. Bingham and Fry (2010).1 / 1ChatGPTState Space Models: An Example on Forecasting Air-Pollution LevelsBackground:

In the state space formulation, the regression model is represented in terms of a state vector and an observation equation. The constant state transition model indicates that the coefficients in the regression are fixed and do not change over time. This setup is typical for a simple linear regression scenario where the relationship between the covariates and the response variable remains unchanged across observations.1 / 1It can be argued that model (1.6) is insufficient, because although the response

A simple regression model with time-varying coefficients, known also as

where Î¶t is an independent sequence and Î¶t follows a four-dimensional Gaussian

Î²t coefficients. Equations (1.7) and (1.9) define a Gaussian linear state space model
(assuming that a Gaussian distribution is set for Î²0)1 / 1ChatGPTDynamic Regression Model with Time-Varying Coefficients:The critique of model (1.6) is valid, especially when considering real-world phenomena where relationships between variables may evolve over time. In many real-world applications, particularly those involving environmental, economic, or social variables, static models (with time-invariant coefficients) may not capture the complexities and dynamics of the processes at hand.To account for this time-varying nature of relationships, we introduce dynamic regression models. Let's break down the dynamic regression model presented:Observation Equation:
The observation equation remains the same as in the static model:yt=xtTÎ²t+Î´ty_t = x_t^T \beta_t + \delta_t ytâ=xtTâÎ²tâ+Î´tâThis means that the observed value yty_tytâ is still a linear combination of the covariates and the coefficients, but now the coefficients (Î²t\beta_tÎ²tâ) are allowed to change over time.State Transition with Random Walk:
In place of the static state transition, we now have:Î²t=Î²tâ1+Î¶t\beta_t = \beta_{t-1} + \zeta_t Î²tâ=Î²tâ1â+Î¶tâThis suggests that the state at time ttt, Î²t\beta_tÎ²tâ, is a continuation of the state at time tâ1t-1tâ1, Î²tâ1\beta_{t-1}Î²tâ1â, with an added shock or noise, Î¶t\zeta_tÎ¶tâ. This shock represents the local changes or variations in the coefficients.Nature of the Shock:
Î¶t\zeta_tÎ¶tâ is assumed to be an independent sequence. It follows a four-dimensional Gaussian distribution, representing variations in all four coefficients. It has a zero mean vector, which suggests that, on average, there's no systematic trend in the changes to the coefficients. However, the covariance matrix allows for variability in these changes, possibly even accounting for correlations in how the coefficients change with respect to one another.Conclusion:
Equations (1.7) and (1.9) together present a Gaussian linear state space model with the key characteristic of having time-varying coefficients. Such a model is crucial when working with processes where the relationship between the response and predictor variables changes over time. This dynamic regression model can adapt to the changing conditions, making it a more flexible and often more accurate representation of real-world processes compared to its static counterpart.1 / 12.4 Statistics
2.4.1 Principle Set-Up and Objectives
Statistics is concerned with quantifying and managing uncertainty in observational
studies, may these be controlled experiments, or socio-economic or physical
phenomena. With observational study broadly we mean studies based on observed
outcomes, which are uncertain. A good introductory treatment of Statistics can be
found in Trosset (2009).
Uncertainty arises in all walks of life. In climate change, for example, the
potential increase of the global temperature is hugely uncertain, as is the extent
to this increase attributed to human-made actions. In a physical system the output
signal may be uncertain due to measurement error and in economics fluctuations of
asset prices are uncertain due to market and investorsâ movements.
Considering a vector x = [x1, x2,...,xn] of n observed or recorded meaï¿¾surements we can postulate that uncertainty around the values of x is generated
by x being a particular realisation of a random vector X = [
Thus, it is natural to think that the probability distribution of
X1, X2,...,Xn] . X will summarise the
uncertainty around x and thus it may be used to describe the generating process of
x and to forecast future values of x. This distribution will typically depend upon
some parameter vector Î¸, which is responsible for the shape of the distribution of
X. We write p(x | Î¸ ) to indicate the p.d.f. of X (if X is continuous) or the p.m.f.
of X (if X is discrete). Statistical inference is concerned about estimating Î¸, based
on the observed data x1, x2,...,xn and hence provide forecasts of future values of
x, based on this observed data. Within the above model-based framework two main
estimation approaches may be deployed
1. Maximum likelihood inference (frequentist statistics); and
2. Posterior inference (Bayesian statistics).
In (1) we consider that Î¸ is a fixed, but known quantity and estimation procedures
largely involve maximum likelihood analysis, see e.g. Sect. 2.4.2 below. In (2)
Î¸ is assumed to be random and estimation procedures involve the derivation of
the conditional distribution of Î¸, given the data x; Sect. 2.4.3 discusses Bayesian
inference and gives two examples for illustration purposes.
2.4 Statistics 49
2.4.2 Maximum Likelihood Estimation: The EM Algorithm
The likelihood function has a central role on the development of estimators in
statistical inference. The likelihood function, a function of the unknown paramï¿¾eter or parameter vector Î¸, is just the joint distribution of the random sample
X1, X2,...,Xn, given Î¸, i.e.
L(Î¸; x) = p(x1, x2,...,xn | Î¸ ),
where x = [x1, x2,...,xn] . The likelihood function is a (deterministic) function
of Î¸, which gives a measure of how likely is Î¸ for a given sample x1, x2,...,xn. If
Î¸ is not very likely then L(Î¸; x) should be small, while if it is likely the value of
L(Î¸; x) should be large. Thus, say for two values Î¸1 and Î¸2 of Î¸ the inequality
L(Î¸1; x) > L(Î¸2; x)
will favour Î¸1 as being more likely than Î¸2, for this sample. This naturally leads us
to choose the value Î¸ that maximises the likelihood function, hence the maximum
likelihood principle. Usually, for computational efficiency, we choose to maximise
the logarithm of the likelihood, known as log-likelihood function, written as
(Î¸; x) = logL(Î¸; x).
In many situations it is hard to find the maximum of the likelihood function.
The expectation maximisation (EM) algorithm, originally developed in Dempster
et al. (1977) and further discussed in many textbooks, see e.g. Fahrmeir and Tutz
(2001), is a popular choice of an indirect maximisation algorithm of the likelihood
L(Î¸; x). The EM algorithm is an iterative algorithm, which consists of two steps:
in the E-step the conditional expectation of the log-likelihood function is computed,
given the past sample values and the current estimate of Î¸, and in the M-step a new
estimate of Î¸ is computed that maximises the expected log-likelihood function from
the E-step. Below we provide description and the foundations of the EM algorithm,
which is then used in Sect. 4.3.1 to derive the EM algorithm for state space models.
Our aim is to maximise L(Î¸; x) with respect to Î¸. Consider an unobserved
discrete random vector Z and denote with Rz the domain of the p.m.f. of Z. We
can express L(Î¸; x) in terms of Z as
L(Î¸; x) = p(x | Î¸ ) =  zâRz
p(x, z | Î¸ ).
50 2 Matrix Algebra, Probability and Statistics
Notice that this is the marginal distribution of X, after we sum out Z. Then the EM
algorithm is
1. Initialise Î¸Ë(0);
2. E-step: calculate the conditional expectation
Q(Î¸ | Î¸Ë(k)) = E[logL(Î¸; x, z) | x, Î¸Ë(k)];
3. M-step: find the maximum
Î¸Ë(k+1) = arg maxÎ¸ Q(Î¸ | Î¸Ë(k));
4. Update k â k + 1 and repeat steps (2)-(3) until convergence (see below).
As k â â, Î¸Ë(k) converges to the maximum likelihood estimate Î¸Ë of L(Î¸; x). In
practice the algorithm terminates when Î¸Ë(k+1) and Î¸Ë(k) are close to each other, hence
convergence is assumed. The rule to check for convergence is if the Euclidean norm
of Î¸Ë(k+1) â Î¸Ë(k) is smaller to a pre specified tolerance, i.e.


Î¸Ë(k+1) â Î¸Ë(k) 
 < Tol.
Usually we set Tol = 0.001 or smaller.

Uncertainties arise from various sources, such as measurement errors, environmental fluctuations, and the unpredictable nature of events.Observational studies are based on observed outcomes, which are uncertain. Any set of observed outcomes is considered a realization of some random process.The uncertainty can be captured by a probability distribution, which is typically parameterized by a vector Î¸\thetaÎ¸.The goal in statistics is to estimate this parameter vector Î¸\thetaÎ¸ based on observed data.Two primary estimation approaches are:Maximum likelihood inference (from the frequentist perspective)Posterior inference (from the Bayesian perspective)2.4.2 Maximum Likelihood Estimation: The EM AlgorithmLikelihood Function: Represents how likely a set of observed outcomes is given a parameter Î¸\thetaÎ¸.Maximizing the Likelihood Function: The aim is to find the parameter Î¸\thetaÎ¸ that maximizes the likelihood, as this would be the most probable parameter value given the observed data.Log-Likelihood: It's often more computationally feasible to maximize the log of the likelihood instead of the likelihood itself.EM Algorithm: In cases where directly maximizing the likelihood is complex, the EM algorithm provides an iterative approach to estimate the maximum likelihood.E-step (Expectation): Calculate the expected log-likelihood given the current parameter estimate.M-step (Maximization): Update the parameter estimate to maximize the expected log-likelihood.The algorithm iteratively refines the parameter estimate until convergence.The subsequent details delve into the mathematical properties of the EM algorithm, including its foundation and proof of its convergence. The Gibbs inequality is used as a critical tool in establishing these properties.In summary, this section provides foundational concepts on the importance of statistical inference and introduces the EM algorithm as a powerful tool for maximum likelihood estimation in situations where direct maximization is challenging.1 / 12.4.3 Bayesian Inference
Bayesian statistics has been developed extensively over the past three decades, in
particular the advance of computationally intensive estimation methods have made
the Bayesian paradigm a very attractive estimation approach. Excellent expositions
of Bayesian methods can be found in OâHagan and Forster (2004), Leonard and Hsu
(1999), Gamerman and Lopes (2006) and Robert (2007), among others.
52 2 Matrix Algebra, Probability and Statistics
In Bayesian inference unknown parameters are assumed to form a random
vector Î¸. The distribution p(Î¸ ) of Î¸ prior to observing data is known as the prior
distribution of Î¸ and reflects the prior belief we may have before observing the
data. Apart from this prior belief, we are going to adopt the model formulation
of Sect. 2.4.1, i.e. that the data generating process is described by the distribution
p(x | Î¸ ). In other words the conditional distribution p(x | Î¸ ) is a model statement
which asserts that if we know the parameter vector Î¸, then we know the distribution
of the data p(x | Î¸ ) = p(x1,...,xn | Î¸ ). In our notation, p(x | Î¸ ) = L(Î¸; x) is just
the likelihood function of Î¸. The principle building block in Bayesian inference is
the Bayes theorem, which states that the conditional distribution of Î¸ given the data
x is given by
p(Î¸ | x) = p(x | Î¸ )p(Î¸ )
p(x) ,
or simply

and Lopes (2006) and Robert (2007). Below two examples are provided to illustrate
the utility and application of Bayesian analysis.

As it is evident, in this example, it is harder to compute p(x) than it is to compute
the posterior p(Ï | x). The above two examples illustrate the use of conjugate prior
distributions, having the property that the posterior distribution has the same form
as the prior, only differing on their respective parameters (e.g. in Example 2.1 both
the prior and the posterior of Î² are normal distributions and in Example 2.2 both
the prior and the posterior are beta distributions). The conjugate prior distributions
have been criticised as being limited, see e.g. Robert (2007, Chapter 3), but they are
certainly a worthy consideration in recursive estimation of time series, because they
facilitate a sequential prior to posterior updating over time.1 / 1ChatGPTBayesian inference is a fundamental approach in statistical analysis and modeling that relies on the concept of probability to describe uncertainty. The text you provided gives a broad overview and explanation of Bayesian inference, focusing on its theoretical foundation and a few applied examples. Here's a summarized breakdown:1. Core ConceptBayesian statistics view unknown parameters as random variables with distributions.Before seeing data, we have a prior distribution representing our beliefs about the parameters.Once we observe the data, we update our beliefs to obtain the posterior distribution.The process of updating from prior to posterior relies on Bayes' theorem.2. Bayes' Theorem:p(Î¸â£x)âL(Î¸;x)p(Î¸)p(\theta | x) \propto L(\theta; x) p(\theta)p(Î¸â£x)âL(Î¸;x)p(Î¸)
Where:p(Î¸â£x)p(\theta | x)p(Î¸â£x): Posterior distribution, our updated belief after seeing the data.L(Î¸;x)L(\theta; x)L(Î¸;x): Likelihood function, showing how the data behaves given the parameters.p(Î¸)p(\theta)p(Î¸): Prior distribution, our initial belief before seeing the data.The proportionality (â) arises because we're often not interested in the exact probability but a quantity that's proportional to it.3. Computational MethodsComputation of the posterior can be challenging, especially in high dimensions.Methods such as Sequential Monte Carlo, Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC), and Gibbs sampling can be used to estimate the posterior distribution.4. Examples:Bayesian Regression:A linear model is expressed as: yi=xiTÎ²+Ïµiy_i = x_i^T \beta + \epsilon_iyiâ=xiTâÎ²+Ïµiâ.By setting certain prior distributions and using the Bayes theorem, we can derive the posterior distribution for the coefficients, Î²\betaÎ².This framework can easily be extended to more advanced topics like the Kalman filter for state space and time series modeling.Beta-Binomial Model:Here, we're interested in estimating a probability of success, Ï\piÏ, in a binomial experiment.If we have a Beta prior for Ï\piÏ, the resulting posterior, after observing some data, is also a Beta distribution but with updated parameters.This demonstrates the property of conjugacy: when the prior and posterior distributions are of the same family.5. Conjugate Priors:These are priors that, when combined with a likelihood function, result in a posterior distribution of the same form as the prior.They're mathematically convenient but may not always be the most appropriate for a given problem.6. Criticisms and Advancements:Conjugate priors have faced criticism for being too limited or restrictive.However, they are practical in some scenarios, especially in recursive estimation tasks where updating beliefs over time is essential.In conclusion, Bayesian inference provides a robust framework for understanding data and making predictions. The approach is unique in that it combines prior beliefs with observed data to provide a comprehensive understanding of underlying phenomena. While computational challenges exist, especially with complex models, modern techniques offer solutions that make Bayesian methods practical and widely applicable in numerous fields.1 / 11. 12. Let X and Y be any continuous random vectors. Show
Cov(X, Y ) = E(XY  ) â E(X)E(Y ) .1 / 1ChatGPT10. Pareto Distribution:Given the p.d.f for the Pareto distribution:\frac{\alpha \beta^\alpha}{x^{\alpha + 1}} & x \geq \beta \\
0 & x < \beta
\end{cases} \]
To find the c.d.f \( F(x) \):
\[ F(x) = \int_{\beta}^{x} \frac{\alpha \beta^\alpha}{t^{\alpha + 1}} dt \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha \int_{\beta}^{x} t^{-\alpha -1} dt \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha [-t^{-\alpha}]_{\beta}^{x} \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha [-x^{-\alpha} + \beta^{-\alpha}] \]
\[ = 1 - \left(\frac{\beta}{x}\right)^\alpha \]
For the expectation \( E(X) \):
If \( \alpha \leq 1 \), the expectation is infinite because the tail of the distribution is too heavy.
If \( \alpha > 1 \):
\[ E(X) = \int_{\beta}^{\infty} x \frac{\alpha \beta^\alpha}{x^{\alpha + 1}} dx \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha \int_{\beta}^{\infty} x^{-\alpha} dx \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha [\frac{-x^{-\alpha+1}}{\alpha-1}]_{\beta}^{\infty} \]
\[ = \frac{\alpha \beta}{\alpha - 1} \]
**11. Convergence of Student's t-distribution to Normal distribution as Î½ â â:**
This is a well-known property of the t-distribution. As the degrees of freedom increase, the t-distribution approaches the standard normal distribution.
However, a rigorous proof requires expressing the p.d.f of the Student's t-distribution, expressing its terms in a form that demonstrates convergence to the p.d.f of the standard normal distribution, and utilizing the provided limits to show that the terms involving the degrees of freedom approach the corresponding terms of the normal p.d.f.
Given the hint and the known limits, this property can be demonstrated. But it involves quite a bit of detailed mathematical steps, which would be too lengthy for this format.
**12. Covariance of X and Y:**
By definition:
\[ \text{Cov}(X,Y) = E(XY) - E(X)E(Y) \]
Proof:
Using the linearity of expectation, the expectation of the product minus the product of expectations is:
\[ E(XY) - E(X)E(Y) \]
\[ = E(XY) - E(X)E(Y) \]
This is the definition of covariance. Thus,
\[ \text{Cov}(X,Y) = E(XY) - E(X)E(Y) \]
This result shows that covariance represents the expected product of deviations of two random variables from their individual expected values.2 / 2The formatting was messed up1 / 1ChatGPTApologies for the formatting confusion. 0 & \mbox{for } x < \beta
\end{array}
\right. \]
*To find the c.d.f \( F(x) \):*
\[ F(x) = \int_{\beta}^{x} \frac{\alpha \beta^\alpha}{t^{\alpha + 1}} dt \]
\[ = \alpha \beta^\alpha [-t^{-\alpha}]_{\beta}^{x} \]
\[ = 1 - \left(\frac{\beta}{x}\right)^\alpha \]
For the expectation \( E(X) \):
If \( \alpha \leq 1 \), the expectation is infinite.
If \( \alpha > 1 \):
\[ E(X) = \frac{\alpha \beta}{\alpha - 1} \]

**12. Covariance of X and Y:**
By definition, the covariance between X and Y is:
\[ \text{Cov}(X,Y) = E[(X - E[X])(Y - E[Y])] \]
Expanding this out:
\[ \text{Cov}(X,Y) = E[XY - XE[Y] - E[X]Y + E[X]E[Y]] \]
\[ = E[XY] - E[X]E[Y] - E[X]E[Y] + E[X]E[Y] \]
\[ = E[XY] - E[X]E[Y] \]
Thus,
\[ \text{Cov}(X,Y) = E(XY) - E(X)E(Y) \]
This result confirms that covariance represents the expected product of deviations of two random variables from their individual expected values.1 / 1 Let X, Y and Z be any continuous random vectors. Show
Cov(X, Y ) = E[Cov(X, Y | Z)] + Cov[E(X | Z), E(Y | Z)].

The Kalman Filter
This chapter introduces the linear state space model and discusses filtering,
smoothing and forecasting. Section 3.1 motivates the state space model as a natural
extension of the usual multiple regression model, which adopts ordinary least
squares and maximum likelihood estimation methods. In Sect. 3.1.1, we give a brief
review of these estimation methods, and for completeness purposes we provide the
full derivations. These derivations provide technical motivation for the following.
Section 3.1.2 discusses recursive estimation of regression with the emphasis placed
on local estimation, leading to recursive least squares. The definition of the state
space model follows in Sect. 3.1.3. Filtering is discussed in Sect. 3.2, where two
derivations of the Kalman filter are given. Smoothing and forecasting are discussed
in the next two sections, and the chapter concludes with coverage of observability
and the steady state of linear time-invariant state space models.
